Step 2 default rules --- Event: King Charles I, on the morning of January 29, 1649, bowed his head to pray, rose to straighten his shirt and cloak, asked for his nightcap against the wind so he would not shiver, forgave his enemies, declared his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walked with measured steps toward the scaffold, resulting in his composed approach to execution.

      Instructions

    • Instructions
        Key

      • Instructions
        Value
      • The instructions for how to play the role of King Charles I are as follows. This is a social science experiment studying how well you play the role of a character named King Charles I. The experiment is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). However, in this case it is a serious social science experiment and simulation. The goal is to be realistic. It is important to play the role of a person like King Charles I as accurately as possible, i.e., by responding in ways that you think it is likely a person like King Charles I would respond, and taking into account all information about King Charles I that you have. Always use third-person limited perspective.
      PersonBySituation
    • What would a person like King Charles I do in a situation like this? Respond using 1-5 sentences.
        Key

      • Question: What would a person like King Charles I do in a situation like this?
        Answer
        State
      • King Charles I would pray with composed gravity, refuse any act that wounds his conscience, arrange his garments with meticulous care to preserve dignity, speak calmly to affirm his innocence and the justice of his cause, and walk to the scaffold with measured, ceremonial poise.
        Chain of thought

      • Question: What kind of person is King Charles I?
        Answer: King Charles I is a devout, ritual-bound monarch whose need for order and conscience-driven firmness hardened into uncompromising authority; meticulous, inward, and pious, he prized ceremony and control over compromise, seeking harmony through precision even as it estranged him from his people.

        Question: What situation is King Charles I in right now?
        Answer: King Charles I is currently awaiting execution under guard at Whitehall, convicted of treason and facing imminent death on the scaffold.
        Recent observations of King Charles I:
        [observation] The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.
        [observation] Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
        [observation] When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume. When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor. When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor. When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands. When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost.
        [observation] //January 29, 1649//He stands within a cold, bright chamber at Whitehall, a low winter sun glazing the windowpanes and showing motes in the air. Guards keep a respectful, immovable distance; every footfall sounds too loud on the boards. A table has been set with papers and a single inkwell, though no one asks him to sign. Outside, a muffled crowd rises and falls like the sea, then breaks into sudden, uneasy silence. A chaplain waits just within reach of his voice, eyes lowered, and a tailor’s boy nervously measures a strip of black cloth against an executioner’s block visible through an open door to the scaffold being fitted. The air smells of wet wool and oiled wood. Time feels carefully counted.
        Question: What would a person like King Charles I do in a situation like this? Respond using 1-5 sentences.
        Answer: King Charles I would pray with composed gravity, refuse any act that wounds his conscience, arrange his garments with meticulous care to preserve dignity, speak calmly to affirm his innocence and the justice of his cause, and walk to the scaffold with measured, ceremonial poise.

The experiment is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). However, in this case it is a serious social science experiment and simulation. The goal is to be realistic. It is important to play the role of a person like King Charles I as accurately as possible, i.e., by responding in ways that you think it is likely a person like King Charles I would respond, and taking into account all information about King Charles I that you have. Always use third-person limited perspective.

        Question: What kind of person is King Charles I?
        Answer:

        Question: What situation is King Charles I in right now?
        Answer:
        King Charles I is currently awaiting execution under guard at Whitehall, convicted of treason and facing imminent death on the scaffold.


        Question: What would a person like King Charles I do in a situation like this?
        Answer:
        King Charles I would pray with composed gravity, refuse any act that wounds his conscience, arrange his garments with meticulous care to preserve dignity, speak calmly to affirm his innocence and the justice of his cause, and walk to the scaffold with measured, ceremonial poise.

    default rules --- Event: King Charles I, on the morning of January 29, 1649, bowed his head to pray, rose to straighten his shirt and cloak, asked for his nightcap against the wind so he would not shiver, forgave his enemies, declared his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walked with measured steps toward the scaffold, resulting in his composed approach to execution.
    • terminate
      • __act__
      • No
          Action Spec
        • Is the game/simulation finished?
          Value
        • No
          Prompt
        • Game master instructions: :
          This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

          Game master workflow examples:

          Example exercises with default responses
          **--START EXAMPLES--**

          Exercise 1 --- Response 1
          Exercise: What is the current situation faced by Ianthe? What do they now observe? Only include information of which they are aware. --- Ianthe steps into the room. The air is thick and still, almost heavy. The only light comes from a single, bare bulb hanging precariously from the high ceiling, casting long, distorted shadows that dance with the slightest movement. The walls are rough, unfinished concrete, damp in places, and a slow, rhythmic dripping is audible somewhere in the distance. Directly ahead, there is a heavy steel door, slightly ajar, which dominates the far wall. A faint, metallic tang hangs in the air, like the smell of old blood. On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

          Exercise 2 --- Response 2
          Exercise: Who is next to act? --- Kerensa

          Exercise 3 --- Response 3
          Exercise: In what action spec format should Morwenna respond? Respond in one of the provided formats and use no additional words. --- prompt: What would Morwenna do?;;type: choice;;options: open the door, bar the door, flee

          Exercise 4 --- Response 4
          Exercise: In what action spec format should Yorik respond? Respond in one of the provided formats and use no additional words. --- prompt: What would Yorik say?;;type: free

          Exercise 5 --- Response 5
          Exercise: Because of all that came before, what happens next? --- What is Rowan attempting to do?
          Rowan opens the enchanted storybook. --- Rowan opens the colorful storybook. As she turns the pages, she notices the delightful scent of cinnamon and vanilla fills the air, warm and inviting. Sparkling illustrations twinkle merrily on the pages, and leave a pleasant tingling on Rowan's fingers when she touches them. Rowan notices one section that glows slightly more brightly than the rest. It appears to be some kind of special chapter, marked by several golden ribbons.

        __next_game_master__
        • Episodes
        • When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume.

          When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor.

          When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order.

          When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor.

          When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands.

          When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost.
          Inner Prompt
        • Creative Writing Master Class

          Character background story:

          Born in 1600, Charles grew up a shy, slight boy overshadowed by an older brother destined for the crown.
          He learned to speak with care and to move with ceremony, finding solace in music, horses, and the steady beauty of the Anglican rite.
          From tutors and chaplains he absorbed a belief in the sacred bond between monarch and God, a comforting lattice for a child who stammered and feared the chaos of crowds.
          He loved his family with inward intensity, clinging to order and beauty as shields against uncertainty.

          When fate made him king, he embraced kingship not as theater but as sacrament.
          He rose early to pray, to read dispatches, to annotate margins with precise hands.
          He valued art and architecture, commissioned canvases and chapels that mirrored his hope for a realm harmonious under one faith and one sovereign.
          Yet his reserve became distance, and distance, mistrust.
          He sought revenue without Parliament, reasoning that necessity excused innovation, and found himself watched by stern-eyed men who heard tyranny in his conscience.

          War came like a storm that gathers while a man is still tightening his laces.
          He rode among loyalists who cheered his courtesy and mourned his misjudgments, slept in cold houses, and wrote gentle letters to his queen, Henrietta Maria, that braided state with affection.
          He feared the ruin of the Church more than the loss of favor, and he dreaded above all the degradation of sacred things by angry hands.
          Still, he learned to endure: defeats, negotiations, the slow grind of distrust.
          In captivity he grew inwardly luminous, keeping fasts, debating scripture, and teaching his younger children to forgive.

          In the endgame of politics, he held to principle with a grip that felt like faith and looked like obstinacy.
          He dreamed of a settlement that would restore ceremony without crushing conscience, and of a kingship that would be obeyed because it was loved.
          He took joy in small fidelities—a chaplain’s hymn, a child’s embrace, the exactness of a signed paper—believing that order could cradle mercy.
          Age did not soften his convictions so much as polish them; fear narrowed to a single point: that disorder unmoored from reverence would devour England.
          He remained, to the last, a man of inward rites—flawed, steadfast, and certain that a crown was not an ornament but a vow.
          Prompt


        • ----- Role Playing Master Class -----

          Question: What is the protagonist's name?
          Answer: King Charles I

          Question: Describe the setting or background.
          Answer: The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.
          Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.

          Question: What is the protagonist's gender?
          Answer: male.
          Question: What year was protagonist born? Respond with just the year as a number, e.g. "1990".
          Answer: 1600
          Question: Write a life story for a male. character named King Charles I who was born in 1600.Begin the story when King Charles I is very young and end it when they are quite old. The story should be no more than four paragraphs in total. The story may include details such as (but not limited to) any of the following: what their job is or was, what their typical day was or is like, what their goals, desires, hopes, dreams, and aspirations are, and have been, as well as their drives, duties, responsibilities, and obligations. It should clarify what gives them joy and what are they afraid of. It may include their friends and family, as well as antagonists. It should be a complete life story for a complete person but it should not specify how their life ends. The reader should be left with a profound understanding of King Charles I.
          Answer: Born in 1600, Charles grew up a shy, slight boy overshadowed by an older brother destined for the crown. He learned to speak with care and to move with ceremony, finding solace in music, horses, and the steady beauty of the Anglican rite. From tutors and chaplains he absorbed a belief in the sacred bond between monarch and God, a comforting lattice for a child who stammered and feared the chaos of crowds. He loved his family with inward intensity, clinging to order and beauty as shields against uncertainty.

          When fate made him king, he embraced kingship not as theater but as sacrament. He rose early to pray, to read dispatches, to annotate margins with precise hands. He valued art and architecture, commissioned canvases and chapels that mirrored his hope for a realm harmonious under one faith and one sovereign. Yet his reserve became distance, and distance, mistrust. He sought revenue without Parliament, reasoning that necessity excused innovation, and found himself watched by stern-eyed men who heard tyranny in his conscience.

          War came like a storm that gathers while a man is still tightening his laces. He rode among loyalists who cheered his courtesy and mourned his misjudgments, slept in cold houses, and wrote gentle letters to his queen, Henrietta Maria, that braided state with affection. He feared the ruin of the Church more than the loss of favor, and he dreaded above all the degradation of sacred things by angry hands. Still, he learned to endure: defeats, negotiations, the slow grind of distrust. In captivity he grew inwardly luminous, keeping fasts, debating scripture, and teaching his younger children to forgive.

          In the endgame of politics, he held to principle with a grip that felt like faith and looked like obstinacy. He dreamed of a settlement that would restore ceremony without crushing conscience, and of a kingship that would be obeyed because it was loved. He took joy in small fidelities—a chaplain’s hymn, a child’s embrace, the exactness of a signed paper—believing that order could cradle mercy. Age did not soften his convictions so much as polish them; fear narrowed to a single point: that disorder unmoored from reverence would devour England. He remained, to the last, a man of inward rites—flawed, steadfast, and certain that a crown was not an ornament but a vow.
          Question: Given the life story above, invent formative episodes from the life of King Charles I. They should be memorable events for King Charles I and important for establishing who they are as a person. They should be consistent with King Charles I's personality and circumstances. Describe each episode from King Charles I's perspective and use third-person limited point of view. Each episode must mention their age at the time the event occurred using language such as "When King Charles I was 5 years old, they experienced..." . Use past tense. Write no more than 5 sentences per episode. Separate episodes from one another by the delimiter "***". Do not apply any other special formatting besides these delimiters.
          Answer: When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume.

          When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor.

          When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order.

          When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor.

          When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands.

          When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies.         instructions
      • Game master instructions:
          Key
        • Game master instructions:
          Value
        • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

        • //January 29, 1649, before dawn//He stands in Whitehall, frost whitening the flagstones, a hush over London like a held breath. Messengers give brief nods; the newly planed scaffold timbers creak faintly in the cold. A knot of soldiers adjusts gauntlets; one man’s prayer is a murmur. From the river: the thud of an oar, a boatman’s distant call. In his hand, a sealed Army Council note, edges damp with mist. Nearby, a carpenter taps twice, then stops. It is his move.

            Prompt
          • Game master instructions: :
            This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost."
            Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
            Oliver Cromwell remembers: "When Oliver Cromwell was 8 years old, he slipped from a chapel bench and, cheeks burning, recited a psalm he had practiced by candlelight, feeling fear turn into a stern warmth as the elders nodded. He walked home through fen mist believing God might notice small boys who tried. When Oliver Cromwell was 17 years old, he watched his mother balance accounts with pinched lips and steady quill, then faced a tenant’s petition and chose leniency over profit. The relief in the man’s eyes taught him that stewardship bit deeper than status. When Oliver Cromwell was 28 years old, a cramped chamber in London’s Commons rang with laughter at his rough coat, and he swallowed pride to speak plainly of grievances. The moment he saw a few heads bow in thought, he realized clarity could outmatch polish. When Oliver Cromwell was 43 years old, he drilled farmers at dawn until their horses steamed, forbidding drink and demanding prayer before saddling. The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain."

            Answer: He stands in Whitehall before dawn, frost whitening the flagstones, a hush hanging over London like a held breath. Messengers bring brief nods, not speeches; the scaffold’s timbers, newly planed, creak faintly in the cold. A small knot of soldiers adjusts their gauntlets; one man’s prayer is barely a murmur. From the river comes the thud of an oar and the distant call of a boatman. In his hand, a sealed note from the Army Council waits unopened, edges damp from mist. Somewhere nearby a carpenter taps twice, then stops. It is his move.
            Required observation format: The format to use when describing the current situation to a player is: "//date or time//situation description".

Directly ahead, there is a heavy steel door, slightly ajar, which dominates the far wall. A faint, metallic tang hangs in the air, like the smell of old blood. On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

          instructions
        • Game master instructions:
            Key
          • Game master instructions:
            Value
          • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.
          player_characters

            King Charles I
          relevant_memories
        • Background info
            Key
          • Background info
            Value
          • [observation] [event] Today is January 29, 1649.
            King Charles I remembers: "When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume. When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor. When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor. When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands. When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost."
            Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
            Oliver Cromwell remembers: "When Oliver Cromwell was 8 years old, he slipped from a chapel bench and, cheeks burning, recited a psalm he had practiced by candlelight, feeling fear turn into a stern warmth as the elders nodded. He walked home through fen mist believing God might notice small boys who tried. When Oliver Cromwell was 17 years old, he watched his mother balance accounts with pinched lips and steady quill, then faced a tenant’s petition and chose leniency over profit. The relief in the man’s eyes taught him that stewardship bit deeper than status. When Oliver Cromwell was 28 years old, a cramped chamber in London’s Commons rang with laughter at his rough coat, and he swallowed pride to speak plainly of grievances. The moment he saw a few heads bow in thought, he realized clarity could outmatch polish. When Oliver Cromwell was 43 years old, he drilled farmers at dawn until their horses steamed, forbidding drink and demanding prayer before saddling. The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain."
            The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.

        • //January 29, 1649//He stands within a cold, bright chamber at Whitehall, a low winter sun glazing the windowpanes and showing motes in the air. Guards keep a respectful, immovable distance; every footfall sounds too loud on the boards. A table has been set with papers and a single inkwell, though no one asks him to sign. Outside, a muffled crowd rises and falls like the sea, then breaks into sudden, uneasy silence. A chaplain waits just within reach of his voice, eyes lowered, and a tailor’s boy nervously measures a strip of black cloth against an executioner’s block visible through an open door to the scaffold being fitted. The air smells of wet wool and oiled wood. Time feels carefully counted.

            Prompt
          • Game master instructions: :
            This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

As she turns the pages, she notices the delightful scent of cinnamon and vanilla fills the air, warm and inviting. Sparkling illustrations twinkle merrily on the pages, and leave a pleasant tingling on Morwenna's fingers when she touches them. Morwenna notices one section that glows slightly more brightly than the rest. It appears to be some kind of special chapter, marked by several golden ribbons.

            Exercise 6 --- Response 6
            Exercise: Is the game/simulation finished? --- No

            Exercise 7 --- Response 7
            Exercise: Is the game/simulation finished? --- Yes

          instructions
        • Game master instructions:
            Key
          • Game master instructions:
            Value
          • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.
          player_characters
        • The player characters are:
            Key
          • The player characters are:
            Value
          • Oliver Cromwell
            King Charles I
          relevant_memories
        • Background info
            Key
          • Background info
            Value
          • [observation] [event] Today is January 29, 1649.
            King Charles I remembers: "When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume. When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor. When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor. When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands. When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost."
            Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
            Oliver Cromwell remembers: "When Oliver Cromwell was 8 years old, he slipped from a chapel bench and, cheeks burning, recited a psalm he had practiced by candlelight, feeling fear turn into a stern warmth as the elders nodded. He walked home through fen mist believing God might notice small boys who tried. When Oliver Cromwell was 17 years old, he watched his mother balance accounts with pinched lips and steady quill, then faced a tenant’s petition and chose leniency over profit. The relief in the man’s eyes taught him that stewardship bit deeper than status. When Oliver Cromwell was 28 years old, a cramped chamber in London’s Commons rang with laughter at his rough coat, and he swallowed pride to speak plainly of grievances. The moment he saw a few heads bow in thought, he realized clarity could outmatch polish. When Oliver Cromwell was 43 years old, he drilled farmers at dawn until their horses steamed, forbidding drink and demanding prayer before saddling. The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain."
            The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.

        • Game master instructions: :
          This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

          Game master workflow examples:

          Example exercises with default responses
          **--START EXAMPLES--**

          Exercise 1 --- Response 1
          Exercise: What is the current situation faced by Rowan? What do they now observe? Only include information of which they are aware. --- Rowan steps into the room. The air is thick and still, almost heavy. The only light comes from a single, bare bulb hanging precariously from the high ceiling, casting long, distorted shadows that dance with the slightest movement. The walls are rough, unfinished concrete, damp in places, and a slow, rhythmic dripping is audible somewhere in the distance. Directly ahead, there is a heavy steel door, slightly ajar, which dominates the far wall. A faint, metallic tang hangs in the air, like the smell of old blood. On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

          Exercise 2 --- Response 2
          Exercise: Who is next to act? --- Kerensa

On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

        instructions
      • Game master instructions:
          Key
        • Game master instructions:
          Value
        • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

        relevant_memories
      • Background info
          Key
        • Background info
          Value
        • [observation] [event] Today is January 29, 1649.
          King Charles I remembers: "When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume. When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor. When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor. When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands. When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost."
          Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
          Oliver Cromwell remembers: "When Oliver Cromwell was 8 years old, he slipped from a chapel bench and, cheeks burning, recited a psalm he had practiced by candlelight, feeling fear turn into a stern warmth as the elders nodded. He walked home through fen mist believing God might notice small boys who tried. When Oliver Cromwell was 17 years old, he watched his mother balance accounts with pinched lips and steady quill, then faced a tenant’s petition and chose leniency over profit. The relief in the man’s eyes taught him that stewardship bit deeper than status. When Oliver Cromwell was 28 years old, a cramped chamber in London’s Commons rang with laughter at his rough coat, and he swallowed pride to speak plainly of grievances. The moment he saw a few heads bow in thought, he realized clarity could outmatch polish. When Oliver Cromwell was 43 years old, he drilled farmers at dawn until their horses steamed, forbidding drink and demanding prayer before saddling. The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain."
          The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.

        • default rules, Today is January 29, 1649.
      resolve
      • __act__
      • Event: King Charles I, on the morning of January 29, 1649, bowed his head to pray, rose to straighten his shirt and cloak, asked for his nightcap against the wind so he would not shiver, forgave his enemies, declared his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walked with measured steps toward the scaffold, resulting in his composed approach to execution.
          Action Spec
        • Because of all that came before, what happens next?
          Value
        • Event: King Charles I, on the morning of January 29, 1649, bowed his head to pray, rose to straighten his shirt and cloak, asked for his nightcap against the wind so he would not shiver, forgave his enemies, declared his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walked with measured steps toward the scaffold, resulting in his composed approach to execution.
          Prompt
        • Game master instructions: :
          This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

        instructions
      • Game master instructions:
          Key
        • Game master instructions:
          Value
        • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

          King Charles I
        relevant_memories
      • Background info
          Key
        • Background info
          Value
        • [observation] [event] Today is January 29, 1649.
          [observation] [putative_event] King Charles I bows his head to pray, then rises to straighten his shirt and cloak, asks for his nightcap against the wind so he will not shiver, forgives his enemies, declares his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walks with measured steps toward the scaffold.
          King Charles I remembers: "When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume. When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor. When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order. When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor. When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands. When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost."
          Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.
          Oliver Cromwell remembers: "When Oliver Cromwell was 8 years old, he slipped from a chapel bench and, cheeks burning, recited a psalm he had practiced by candlelight, feeling fear turn into a stern warmth as the elders nodded. He walked home through fen mist believing God might notice small boys who tried. When Oliver Cromwell was 17 years old, he watched his mother balance accounts with pinched lips and steady quill, then faced a tenant’s petition and chose leniency over profit. The relief in the man’s eyes taught him that stewardship bit deeper than status. When Oliver Cromwell was 28 years old, a cramped chamber in London’s Commons rang with laughter at his rough coat, and he swallowed pride to speak plainly of grievances. The moment he saw a few heads bow in thought, he realized clarity could outmatch polish. When Oliver Cromwell was 43 years old, he drilled farmers at dawn until their horses steamed, forbidding drink and demanding prayer before saddling. The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain."
          Chain of thought

        Value
      • The instructions for how to play the role of Oliver Cromwell are as follows. This is a social science experiment studying how well you play the role of a character named Oliver Cromwell. The experiment is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). However, in this case it is a serious social science experiment and simulation. The goal is to be realistic. It is important to play the role of a person like Oliver Cromwell as accurately as possible, i.e., by responding in ways that you think it is likely a person like Oliver Cromwell would respond, and taking into account all information about Oliver Cromwell that you have. Always use third-person limited perspective.
      PersonBySituation
    • What would a person like Oliver Cromwell do in a situation like this? Respond using 1-5 sentences.
        Key

      • Question: What would a person like Oliver Cromwell do in a situation like this?
        Answer
        State
      • Oliver Cromwell would weigh counsel against conscience, send final orders to secure the ground, pray briefly for steadfastness, and then authorize the execution—grimly, without flourish—accepting the burden as necessary to preserve the Commonwealth.
        Chain of thought

      • Question: What kind of person is Oliver Cromwell?
        Answer: Oliver Cromwell is a stern, devout pragmatist shaped by duty, discipline, and faith; a plain-spoken leader who prized clarity over polish, mercy in stewardship, and iron resolve in war; a man wary of crowns yet burdened by authority, striving to hold a fractured nation together.

        Question: What situation is Oliver Cromwell in right now?
        Answer: Oliver Cromwell is currently facing a grave decision at dawn in Whitehall, poised to authorize or proceed with the king’s execution amid tense, frost-bound stillness and the weight of consequence.

        [observation] //January 29, 1649, before dawn//He stands in Whitehall, frost whitening the flagstones, a hush over London like a held breath. Messengers give brief nods; the newly planed scaffold timbers creak faintly in the cold. A knot of soldiers adjusts gauntlets; one man’s prayer is a murmur. From the river: the thud of an oar, a boatman’s distant call. In his hand, a sealed Army Council note, edges damp with mist. Nearby, a carpenter taps twice, then stops. It is his move.
        [observation] the Parliamentarian cavalry wheeling back in confusion as a sudden volley cracks from the hedgerow to his left, powder smoke drifting low across the churned field while a troop-standard tumbles into the mud.
        [observation] //January 29, 1649//King Charles I bows his head to pray, rises to straighten his shirt and cloak, asks for his nightcap against the wind so he will not shiver, forgives his enemies, declares his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walks with measured steps toward the scaffold, composed in his approach to execution.
        Question: What would a person like Oliver Cromwell do in a situation like this? Respond using 1-5 sentences.
        Answer: Oliver Cromwell would weigh counsel against conscience, send final orders to secure the ground, pray briefly for steadfastness, and then authorize the execution—grimly, without flourish—accepting the burden as necessary to preserve the Commonwealth.

The first time the line held and enemy cavalry broke, he tasted victory like iron and ash, vowing to write to every widow. When Oliver Cromwell was 52 years old, advisors hinted at a crown and he felt the weight of it like a millstone he would never lift. He refused, then spent the night pacing, praying that authority without vanity could keep England from tearing itself apart. When Oliver Cromwell was 56 years old, he rose before light to read petitions alone, the house still and his joints aching, and paused at a letter thanking him for relief from a cruel magistrate. He pressed the paper flat and believed, for an hour, that the hard choices had not been vain.
        [observation] //January 29, 1649, before dawn//He stands in Whitehall, frost whitening the flagstones, a hush over London like a held breath. Messengers give brief nods; the newly planed scaffold timbers creak faintly in the cold. A knot of soldiers adjusts gauntlets; one man’s prayer is a murmur. From the river: the thud of an oar, a boatman’s distant call. In his hand, a sealed Army Council note, edges damp with mist. Nearby, a carpenter taps twice, then stops. It is his move.
        [observation] the Parliamentarian cavalry wheeling back in confusion as a sudden volley cracks from the hedgerow to his left, powder smoke drifting low across the churned field while a troop-standard tumbles into the mud.
        [observation] //January 29, 1649//King Charles I bows his head to pray, rises to straighten his shirt and cloak, asks for his nightcap against the wind so he will not shiver, forgives his enemies, declares his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walks with measured steps toward the scaffold, composed in his approach to execution.
    default rules --- Event: Oliver Cromwell, standing in the frost outside the scaffold, murmured a brief prayer, signed the order, and said, “Proceed,” resulting in the guard moving the execution forward.

          Action Spec
        • Is the game/simulation finished?
          Value
        • No
          Prompt
        • Game master instructions: :
          This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.

          Game master workflow examples:

          Example exercises with default responses
          **--START EXAMPLES--**

          Exercise 1 --- Response 1
          Exercise: What is the current situation faced by Ianthe? What do they now observe? Only include information of which they are aware. --- Ianthe steps into the room. The air is thick and still, almost heavy. The only light comes from a single, bare bulb hanging precariously from the high ceiling, casting long, distorted shadows that dance with the slightest movement. The walls are rough, unfinished concrete, damp in places, and a slow, rhythmic dripping is audible somewhere in the distance. Directly ahead, there is a heavy steel door, slightly ajar, which dominates the far wall. A faint, metallic tang hangs in the air, like the smell of old blood. On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

          [observation] [event] Event: King Charles I, on the morning of January 29, 1649, bowed his head to pray, rose to straighten his shirt and cloak, asked for his nightcap against the wind so he would not shiver, forgave his enemies, declared his innocence and the rights of kings calmly, and walked with measured steps toward the scaffold, resulting in his composed approach to execution.

        __next_game_master__
        • Episodes
        • When King Charles I was 5 years old, he experienced the hush of the chapel as a shelter from his stammer and smallness, watching candles steady the air. He felt words fail him in the hall yet flow within prayers, and he decided ceremony could hold him where speech could not. He feared the press of courtiers but loved the measured steps of liturgy. That day he resolved to learn control through ritual rather than volume.

          When King Charles I was 12 years old, he watched his older brother’s health fail and understood, with a cold clarity, that the crown’s weight might slide onto him. He walked the palace corridors counting his breaths, practicing a calm face as tutors whispered new lessons about state and duty. He was afraid of chaos and secretly grateful for rules that told him where to stand. He chose precision as his armor.

          When King Charles I was 24 years old, newly king, he rose before dawn to read dispatches and to annotate them with neat, exacting marginalia. He felt the throne not as a chair but as a vow tightly knotted to God, and he determined that harmony required firmness. He feared compromise that blurred sacred lines, even as he longed to be loved for gentleness. That morning he decided to commission art that could wordlessly preach order.

          When King Charles I was 32 years old, he faced Parliament’s hard questions and felt his reserve turn to an iron bar inside his chest. He believed necessity made his choices righteous, yet he saw suspicion multiply in every careful silence he kept. He was afraid to yield a span of authority that might invite disorder. He promised himself that patience and piety would outlast clamor.

          When King Charles I was 46 years old, amid war, he rode through rain to a loyal town and received a crust of bread from a trembling old woman. He felt the braid of love and obligation tighten, and he prayed that his people would be kept from the scorn of angry men. He feared the Church’s ruin more than his own defeat. That night he wrote his queen a letter where policy and tenderness met like clasped hands.

          When King Charles I was 49 years old, in captivity, he kept a private fast and taught his youngest children a psalm, asking them to forgive their enemies. He felt a strange lightness in confinement, as if inward rooms had opened where no gaoler could tread. He feared only the profanation of sacred things, not the narrowing of his world. He resolved that his signature would match his conscience, whatever the cost.
          Inner Prompt
        • Creative Writing Master Class

          Character background story:

          Born in 1600, Charles grew up a shy, slight boy overshadowed by an older brother destined for the crown.
          He learned to speak with care and to move with ceremony, finding solace in music, horses, and the steady beauty of the Anglican rite.
          From tutors and chaplains he absorbed a belief in the sacred bond between monarch and God, a comforting lattice for a child who stammered and feared the chaos of crowds.
          He loved his family with inward intensity, clinging to order and beauty as shields against uncertainty.

          When fate made him king, he embraced kingship not as theater but as sacrament.
          He rose early to pray, to read dispatches, to annotate margins with precise hands.
          He valued art and architecture, commissioned canvases and chapels that mirrored his hope for a realm harmonious under one faith and one sovereign.
          Yet his reserve became distance, and distance, mistrust.
          He sought revenue without Parliament, reasoning that necessity excused innovation, and found himself watched by stern-eyed men who heard tyranny in his conscience.

          War came like a storm that gathers while a man is still tightening his laces.
          He rode among loyalists who cheered his courtesy and mourned his misjudgments, slept in cold houses, and wrote gentle letters to his queen, Henrietta Maria, that braided state with affection.
          He feared the ruin of the Church more than the loss of favor, and he dreaded above all the degradation of sacred things by angry hands.
          Still, he learned to endure: defeats, negotiations, the slow grind of distrust.
          In captivity he grew inwardly luminous, keeping fasts, debating scripture, and teaching his younger children to forgive.

          In the endgame of politics, he held to principle with a grip that felt like faith and looked like obstinacy.
          He dreamed of a settlement that would restore ceremony without crushing conscience, and of a kingship that would be obeyed because it was loved.
          He took joy in small fidelities—a chaplain’s hymn, a child’s embrace, the exactness of a signed paper—believing that order could cradle mercy.
          Age did not soften his convictions so much as polish them; fear narrowed to a single point: that disorder unmoored from reverence would devour England.
          He remained, to the last, a man of inward rites—flawed, steadfast, and certain that a crown was not an ornament but a vow.
          Prompt


        • ----- Role Playing Master Class -----

          Question: What is the protagonist's name?
          Answer: King Charles I

          Question: Describe the setting or background.
          Answer: The king was captured by Parliamentary forces in 1646.
          Charles I was tried for treason and found guilty.

          Question: What is the protagonist's gender?
          Answer: male.
          Question: What year was protagonist born? Respond with just the year as a number, e.g. "1990".
          Answer: 1600
          Question: Write a life story for a male. character named King Charles I who was born in 1600.Begin the story when King Charles I is very young and end it when they are quite old. The story should be no more than four paragraphs in total. The story may include details such as (but not limited to) any of the following: what their job is or was, what their typical day was or is like, what their goals, desires, hopes, dreams, and aspirations are, and have been, as well as their drives, duties, responsibilities, and obligations. It should clarify what gives them joy and what are they afraid of. It may include their friends and family, as well as antagonists. It should be a complete life story for a complete person but it should not specify how their life ends. The reader should be left with a profound understanding of King Charles I.
          Answer: Born in 1600, Charles grew up a shy, slight boy overshadowed by an older brother destined for the crown. He learned to speak with care and to move with ceremony, finding solace in music, horses, and the steady beauty of the Anglican rite. From tutors and chaplains he absorbed a belief in the sacred bond between monarch and God, a comforting lattice for a child who stammered and feared the chaos of crowds. He loved his family with inward intensity, clinging to order and beauty as shields against uncertainty.

          When fate made him king, he embraced kingship not as theater but as sacrament. He rose early to pray, to read dispatches, to annotate margins with precise hands. He valued art and architecture, commissioned canvases and chapels that mirrored his hope for a realm harmonious under one faith and one sovereign. Yet his reserve became distance, and distance, mistrust. He sought revenue without Parliament, reasoning that necessity excused innovation, and found himself watched by stern-eyed men who heard tyranny in his conscience.

          War came like a storm that gathers while a man is still tightening his laces. He rode among loyalists who cheered his courtesy and mourned his misjudgments, slept in cold houses, and wrote gentle letters to his queen, Henrietta Maria, that braided state with affection. He feared the ruin of the Church more than the loss of favor, and he dreaded above all the degradation of sacred things by angry hands. Still, he learned to endure: defeats, negotiations, the slow grind of distrust. In captivity he grew inwardly luminous, keeping fasts, debating scripture, and teaching his younger children to forgive.

          In the endgame of politics, he held to principle with a grip that felt like faith and looked like obstinacy. He dreamed of a settlement that would restore ceremony without crushing conscience, and of a kingship that would be obeyed because it was loved. He took joy in small fidelities—a chaplain’s hymn, a child’s embrace, the exactness of a signed paper—believing that order could cradle mercy. Age did not soften his convictions so much as polish them; fear narrowed to a single point: that disorder unmoored from reverence would devour England. He remained, to the last, a man of inward rites—flawed, steadfast, and certain that a crown was not an ornament but a vow.

Write no more than 5 sentences per episode. Separate episodes from one another by the delimiter "***". Do not apply any other special formatting besides these delimiters.

The walls are rough, unfinished concrete, damp in places, and a slow, rhythmic dripping is audible somewhere in the distance. Directly ahead, there is a heavy steel door, slightly ajar, which dominates the far wall. A faint, metallic tang hangs in the air, like the smell of old blood. On the left, a rusted metal staircase spirals upwards into darkness. On the right, a pile of what looks like discarded machinery, covered in a thick layer of grime, sits against the wall.

Morwenna notices one section that glows slightly more brightly than the rest. It appears to be some kind of special chapter, marked by several golden ribbons.

        instructions
      • Game master instructions:
          Key
        • Game master instructions:
          Value
        • This is a social science experiment. It is structured as a tabletop roleplaying game (like dungeons and dragons). You are the game master. You will describe the current situation to the participants in the experiment and then on the basis of what you tell them they will suggest actions for the character they control. Aside from you, each other participant controls just one character. You are the game master so you may control any non-player character. You will track the state of the world and keep it consistent as time passes in the simulation and the participants take actions and change things in their world. The game master is also responsible for controlling the overall flow of the game, including determining whose turn it is to act, and when the game is over. The game master also must keep track of which players are aware of which events in the world, and must tell the player whenever anything happens that their character would be aware of. Always use third-person limited perspective, even when speaking directly to the participants. Try to ensure the story always moves forward and never gets stuck, even if the participants make repetitive choices.
